<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>The Sun</i> (United Kingdom)</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>

<p><i><b>The Sun</b></i> is a daily <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_(newspaper_format)" title="Tabloid (newspaper format)">tabloid</a> newspaper published in the United Kingdom and Ireland. Since <i>The Sun on Sunday</i> was launched in February 2012, the paper has been a seven-day operation. As a broadsheet, it was founded in 1964 as a successor to the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Herald_(UK_newspaper)" title="Daily Herald (UK newspaper)">Daily Herald</a></i>; it became a tabloid in 1969 after it was purchased by its current owners. It is published by the <a href="/wiki/News_UK#News_Group_Newspapers_Ltd" title="News UK">News Group Newspapers</a> division of <a href="/wiki/News_UK" title="News UK">News UK</a>, itself a wholly owned <a href="/wiki/Subsidiary" title="Subsidiary">subsidiary</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Murdoch" title="Rupert Murdoch">Rupert Murdoch</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/News_Corp_(company)" title="News Corp (company)">News Corp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-circ_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-circ-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Sun</i> had the largest <a href="/wiki/Newspaper_circulation" title="Newspaper circulation">circulation</a> of any daily newspaper in the United Kingdom,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-circ_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-circ-6">[6]</a></sup> but in late 2013 slipped to second largest Saturday newspaper behind the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mail_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mail-8">[8]</a></sup> It had an average daily circulation of 2.2 million copies in March 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-circ_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-circ-6">[6]</a></sup> Between July and December 2013 the paper had an average daily readership of approximately 5.5 million, with approximately 31% of those falling into the <a href="/wiki/NRS_social_grade" title="NRS social grade">ABC1</a> demographic and 68% in the <a href="/wiki/NRS_social_grade" title="NRS social grade">C2DE</a> demographic. Approximately 41% of readers are women and 59% are men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-circ_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-circ-6">[6]</a></sup> <i>The Sun</i> has been involved in many controversies in its history, including its coverage of the 1989 <a href="/wiki/Hillsborough_disaster" title="Hillsborough disaster">Hillsborough football stadium disaster</a>. Regional editions of the newspaper for <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> are published in Glasgow (<i><b>The Scottish Sun</b></i>), Belfast (<i><b>The Sun</b></i>) and Dublin (<i><b>The Irish Sun</b></i>) respectively.</p>
<p>On 26 February 2012, <i><b>The Sun on Sunday</b></i> was launched to replace the closed <i><a href="/wiki/News_of_the_World" title="News of the World">News of the World</a></i>, employing some of its former journalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuardRMemail_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuardRMemail-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The average circulation for <i>The Sun on Sunday</i> in March 2014 was 1,686,840;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sunday_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sunday-12">[12]</a></sup> but in May 2015 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mail_on_Sunday" title="The Mail on Sunday">The Mail on Sunday</a></i> sold more copies for the first time, an average of 28,650 over those of its rival: 1,497,855 to 1,469,195.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roy_Greenslade" title="Roy Greenslade">Roy Greenslade</a> issued some caveats over the May 2015 figures, but believes the weekday <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i> will overtake <i>The Sun</i> in circulation during 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Sun_before_Murdoch"><i>The Sun</i> before Murdoch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The Sun before Murdoch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>The Sun</i> was first published as a <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheet</a> on 15 September 1964,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> with a logo featuring a glowing orange disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It was launched by owners <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPC_Media" title="IPC Media">IPC</a> (International Publishing Corporation) to replace the failing <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Herald_(UK_newspaper)" title="Daily Herald (UK newspaper)">Daily Herald</a></i>.</p>
<p>Research commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Harmsworth_King" title="Cecil Harmsworth King">Cecil King</a> from <a href="/wiki/Mark_Abrams" title="Mark Abrams">Mark Abrams</a> of <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sussex" title="University of Sussex">Sussex University</a>, <i>The Newspaper Reading Public of Tomorrow</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> identified demographic changes which suggested reasons why the <i>Herald</i> might be in decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The new paper was intended to add a readership of "social radicals" to the <i>Herald</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> "political radicals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curran_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curran-19">[19]</a></sup> Supposedly there was "an immense, sophisticated and superior middle class, hitherto undetected and yearning for its own newspaper", wrote Bernard Shrimsley of Abrams' work forty years later. "As delusions go, this was in the El Dorado class".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shrimsley2004_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shrimsley2004-20">[20]</a></sup> Launched with an advertising budget of £400,000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandbrook58_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandbrook58-21">[21]</a></sup> the brash new paper "burst forth with tremendous energy", according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Times_1964.2C_pp._10-11_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Times_1964.2C_pp._10-11-22">[22]</a></sup> Its initial print run of 3.5 million was attributed to "curiosity" and the "advantage of novelty",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Times_1964.2C_pp._10-11_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Times_1964.2C_pp._10-11-22">[22]</a></sup> and had declined to the previous circulation of the <i>Daily Herald</i> (1.2 million)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curran_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Curran-19">[19]</a></sup> within a few weeks.</p>
<p>By 1969, according to <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Cudlipp" title="Hugh Cudlipp">Hugh Cudlipp</a>, <i>The Sun</i> was losing about £2m a year<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grundy-23">[23]</a></sup> and had a circulation of 800,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandbrook58_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sandbrook58-21">[21]</a></sup> IPC decided to sell to stop the losses, according to Bernard Shrimsley in 2004, out of a fear that the unions would disrupt publication of the <i>Mirror</i> if they did not continue to publish the original <i>Sun</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shrimsley2004_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shrimsley2004-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bill_Grundy" title="Bill Grundy">Bill Grundy</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spectator" title="The Spectator">The Spectator</a></i> in July 1969 that although it published "fine writers" in <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Goodman" title="Geoffrey Goodman">Geoffrey Goodman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Banks-Smith" title="Nancy Banks-Smith">Nancy Banks-Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Akass" title="John Akass">John Akass</a> among others, it had never overcome the negative impact of its launch at which it still resembled the <i>Herald</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grundy-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Book publisher and <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Member of Parliament</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Maxwell" title="Robert Maxwell">Robert Maxwell</a>, eager to buy a British newspaper, offered to take it off their hands and retain its commitment to the Labour Party, but admitted there would be redundancies, especially among the printers. <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Murdoch" title="Rupert Murdoch">Rupert Murdoch</a>, meanwhile, had bought the <i><a href="/wiki/News_of_the_World" title="News of the World">News of the World</a></i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensationalist" title="Sensationalist">sensationalist</a> Sunday newspaper, the previous year, but the presses in the basement of his building in London's <a href="/wiki/Bouverie_Street" title="Bouverie Street">Bouverie Street</a> were unused six days a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stokes_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stokes-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Seizing the opportunity to increase his presence on Fleet Street, he made an agreement with the print unions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stokes_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stokes-24">[24]</a></sup> promising fewer redundancies if he acquired the newspaper. He assured IPC that he would publish a "straightforward, honest newspaper" which would continue to support Labour. IPC, under pressure from the unions, rejected Maxwell's offer, and Murdoch bought the paper for £800,000, to be paid in instalments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenslade.2C_Ch._9_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade.2C_Ch._9-25">[25]</a></sup> He would later remark: "I am constantly amazed at the ease with which I entered British newspapers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChippindaleCh1_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChippindaleCh1-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Daily Herald</i> had been printed in Manchester since 1930, as was the <i>Sun</i> after its original launch in 1964, but Murdoch stopped publication there in 1969 which put the ageing Bouverie Street presses under extreme pressure as circulation grew.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Murdoch_years">Early Murdoch years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early Murdoch years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Murdoch found he had such a rapport with <a href="/wiki/Larry_Lamb_(newspaper_editor)" title="Larry Lamb (newspaper editor)">Larry Lamb</a> over lunch that other potential recruits as editor were not interviewed and Lamb was appointed as the first editor of the new <i>Sun</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph2000_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph2000-27">[27]</a></sup> Lamb wanted <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Shrimsley" title="Bernard Shrimsley">Bernard Shrimsley</a> to be his deputy, which Murdoch accepted as Shrimsley had been the second name on his list of preferences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times2016_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Times2016-28">[28]</a></sup> Lamb was scathing in his opinion of the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mirror" title="Daily Mirror">Daily Mirror</a></i>, where he had recently been employed as a senior sub-editor, and shared Murdoch's view that a paper's quality was best measured by its sales, and he regarded the <i>Mirror</i> as overstaffed, and primarily aimed at an ageing readership. Lamb hastily recruited a staff of about 125 reporters, who were mostly selected for their availability rather than their ability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChippindaleCh1_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChippindaleCh1-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>This was about a quarter of what the <i>Mirror</i> then employed, and Murdoch had to draft in staff on loan from his Australian papers. Murdoch immediately relaunched <i>The Sun</i> as a <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_(newspaper_format)" title="Tabloid (newspaper format)">tabloid</a>, and ran it as a sister paper to the <i>News of the World</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChippindaleCh1_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-ChippindaleCh1-26">[26]</a></sup> <i>The Sun</i> used the same printing presses, and the two papers were managed together at senior executive levels.</p>
<p>The new tabloid <i>Sun</i> was first published on 17 November 1969, with a front page headlined "HORSE DOPE SENSATION", an ephemeral "exclusive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> An editorial on page 2 announced: "Today's <i>Sun</i> is a new newspaper. It has a new shape, new writers, new ideas. But it inherits all that is best from the great traditions of its predecessors. <i>The Sun</i> cares. About the quality of life. About the kind of world we live in. And about people". The first issue had an "exclusive interview" with the Labour Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>, on page 9.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The paper copied the rival <i>Daily Mirror</i> in several ways. It was the same size and its masthead had the title in white on a red rectangle of the same colour as the <i>Daily Mirror</i>. The <i>Mirror</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> "Live Letters" was matched by "Livelier Letters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sex was used as an important element in the content and marketing the paper from the start, which Lamb believed was the most important part of his readers' lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph2000_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph2000-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The first topless <a href="/wiki/Page_3" title="Page 3">Page 3</a> model appeared on 17 November 1970, German-born <a href="/wiki/Stephanie_Marrian" title="Stephanie Marrian">Stephanie Rahn</a>; she was tagged as a "Birthday Suit Girl" to mark the first anniversary of the relaunched <i>Sun</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.26H_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-C.26H-33">[33]</a></sup> A topless Page 3 model gradually became a regular fixture, and with increasingly risqué poses. Both feminists and many cultural conservatives saw the pictures as pornographic and misogynistic. Lamb later expressed some regret at introducing the feature, although denied it was sexist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph2000_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph2000-27">[27]</a></sup> A Conservative council in Sowerby Bridge, Yorkshire, was the first to ban the paper from its public library, shortly after Page 3 began, because of its excessive sexual content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.26H47_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-C.26H47-34">[34]</a></sup> Shrimsley, Lamb's deputy, came up with the headline, "The Silly Burghers of Sowerby Bridge" to describe the councillors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times2016_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Times2016-28">[28]</a></sup> The decision was reversed after a sustained campaign by the newspaper itself lasting 16 months, and the election of a Labour-led council in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.26H47_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-C.26H47-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HorrieBBC_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-HorrieBBC-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Alex_Lyon" title="Alex Lyon">Alex Lyon</a> waved a copy of <i>The Sun</i> in the House of Commons and suggested the paper could be prosecuted for indecency. Sexually related features such as "Do Men Still Want To Marry A Virgin?" and "The Way into a Woman's Bed" began to appear. Serialisations of erotic books were frequent; the publication of extracts from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sensuous_Woman" title="The Sensuous Woman">The Sensuous Woman</a></i>, at a time when copies of the book were being seized by Customs, produced a scandal and a significant amount of free publicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.26H32_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-C.26H32-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Politically, <i>The Sun</i> in the early Murdoch years remained nominally Labour-supporting. It advocated a vote for the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> in the 1970 General Election,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-37">[37]</a></sup> with the headline "Why It Must Be Labour",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> but by February 1974 it was calling for a vote for the Conservative Party led by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> while suggesting that it might support a Labour Party led by <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Roy Jenkins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-37">[37]</a></sup> In the October election an editorial asserted: "ALL our instincts are left rather than right and we would vote for any able politician who would describe himself as a Social Democrat."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-37">[37]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum,_1975" title="United Kingdom European Communities membership referendum, 1975">1975 referendum</a> on Britain continuing membership of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a>, it advocated a vote to stay in the Common Market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The editor, Larry Lamb, was originally from a Labour background with a socialist upbringing, while his temporary replacement Bernard Shrimsley (1972–75) was a middle-class uncommitted <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a>. An extensive advertising campaign on the <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">ITV</a> network in this period, voiced by actor <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Timothy" title="Christopher Timothy">Christopher Timothy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> may have helped <i>The Sun</i> to overtake the <i>Daily Mirror</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> circulation in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Despite the industrial relations of the 1970s – the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Spanish_practices" title="Spanish practices">Spanish practices</a>" of the print unions – <i>The Sun</i> was very profitable, enabling Murdoch to expand his operations to the <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_the_United_States" title="List of newspapers in the United States">United States</a> from 1973.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thatcher_years">Thatcher years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Thatcher years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes">Changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1979 the paper endorsed <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> in the year's <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">general election</a>, at the end of a process which had been under way for some time, though <i>The Sun</i> had not initially been enthusiastic for Thatcher. On 3 May 1979, it ran the unequivocal front page headline, "VOTE TORY THIS TIME".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Star_(United_Kingdom)" title="Daily Star (United Kingdom)">Daily Star</a></i> had been launched in 1978 by Express Newspapers, and by 1981 had begun to affect sales of <i>The Sun</i>. So <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bingo_(Commonwealth)" title="Bingo (Commonwealth)">bingo</a> was introduced as a marketing tool and a 2p drop in cover price removed the <i>Daily Star</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s competitive advantage opening a new circulation battle which resulted in <i>The Sun</i> neutralising the threat of the new paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Page331_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Page331-43">[43]</a></sup> The new editor of <i>The Sun</i>, <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_MacKenzie" title="Kelvin MacKenzie">Kelvin MacKenzie</a>, took up his post in 1981 just after these developments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and "changed the British tabloid concept more profoundly than [Larry] Lamb did", according to Bruce Page; under MacKenzie<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Page331_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Page331-43">[43]</a></sup> the paper became "more outrageous, opinionated and irreverent than anything ever produced in Britain".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Falklands_War">The Falklands War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Falklands War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_(Gotcha).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="284" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/The_Sun_%28Gotcha%29.png/170px-The_Sun_%28Gotcha%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/The_Sun_%28Gotcha%29.png/255px-The_Sun_%28Gotcha%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/58/The_Sun_%28Gotcha%29.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_(Gotcha).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Front page of <i>The Sun</i> (4 May 1982) in early editions following the torpedoing of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARA_General_Belgrano" title="ARA General Belgrano">Belgrano</a>.</i> This headline was published before it was known the sinking of the vessel had cost 368 lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Page333_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Page333-46">[46]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>The Sun</i> became an ardent supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>. The coverage "captured the zeitgeist", according to <a href="/wiki/Roy_Greenslade" title="Roy Greenslade">Roy Greenslade</a>, assistant editor at the time (though privately an opponent of the war), but was also "xenophobic, bloody-minded, ruthless, often reckless, black-humoured and ultimately triumphalist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenslade2002_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade2002-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 May, <i>The Sun</i> claimed to have "<a href="/wiki/Sponsor_(commercial)" title="Sponsor (commercial)">sponsored</a>" a British missile. Under the headline "Stick This Up Your Junta: A Sun missile for Galtieri’s gauchos",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> the newspaper published a <a href="/wiki/Photograph" title="Photograph">photograph</a> of a missile (actually a <a href="/wiki/UGM-27_Polaris" title="UGM-27 Polaris">Polaris missile</a> stock shot from the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Defence (United Kingdom)">Ministry of Defence</a>) which had a large <i>Sun</i> logo printed on its side with the caption "Here It Comes, Senors..." underneath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenslade2002_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade2002-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chippindale138_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chippindale138-49">[49]</a></sup> The paper explained that it was "sponsoring" the missile by contributing to the eventual victory party on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Invincible_(R05)" title="HMS Invincible (R05)">HMS <i>Invincible</i></a> when the war ended. In copy written by <a href="/wiki/Wendy_Henry" title="Wendy Henry">Wendy Henry</a>, the paper said that the missile would shortly be used against Argentinian forces. Tony Snow, <i>The Sun</i> journalist on <i>Invincible</i> who had "signed" the missile, reported a few days later that it had hit an Argentinian target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenslade2002_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade2002-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chippindale138_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chippindale138-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the paper's best known front pages, published on 4 May 1982, commemorated the torpedoing of the Argentine ship the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARA_General_Belgrano" title="ARA General Belgrano">General Belgrano</a></i> by running the story under the headline "GOTCHA".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BJR_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-BJR-50">[50]</a></sup> At MacKenzie's insistence, and against the wishes of Murdoch (the mogul was present because almost all the journalists were on strike),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenslade445_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade445-51">[51]</a></sup> the headline was changed for later editions after the extent of Argentinian casualties became known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Page333_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Page333-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChippindaleCh7_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChippindaleCh7-52">[52]</a></sup> John Shirley, a reporter for <i>The Sunday Times</i>, witnessed copies of this edition of <i>The Sun</i> being thrown overboard by sailors and marines on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Fearless_(L10)" title="HMS Fearless (L10)">HMS <i>Fearless</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenslade445_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade445-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/HMS_Sheffield_(D80)" title="HMS Sheffield (D80)">HMS <i>Sheffield</i></a> was wrecked by an Argentinian attack, <i>The Sun</i> was heavily criticised and even mocked for its coverage of the war in <i>The Daily Mirror</i> and <i>The Guardian</i>, and the wider media queried the veracity of official information and worried about the number of casualties, <i>The Sun</i> gave its response. "There are traitors in our midst", wrote leader writer Ronald Spark on 7 May, accusing commentators on <i>Daily Mirror</i> and <i>The Guardian</i>, plus the BBC's defence correspondent <a href="/wiki/Peter_Snow" title="Peter Snow">Peter Snow</a>, of "treason" for aspects of their coverage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satirical" title="Satirical">satirical</a> magazine <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_Eye_(magazine)" title="Private Eye (magazine)">Private Eye</a></i> mocked and lampooned what they regarded as the paper's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jingoistic" title="Jingoistic">jingoistic</a> coverage, most memorably with the mock-<i>Sun</i> headline "KILL AN ARGIE, WIN A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rover_Metro" title="Rover Metro">METRO</a>!", to which MacKenzie is said to have jokingly responded, "Why didn't we think of that?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Sun_and_the_Labour_Party"><i>The Sun</i> and the Labour Party</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The Sun and the Labour Party">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>These years included what was called "spectacularly malicious coverage"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> of the Labour Party by <i>The Sun</i> and other newspapers. During the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">general election of 1983</a> <i>The Sun</i> ran a front page featuring an unflattering photograph of <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a>, then aged almost 70, claiming he was unfit to be Prime Minister on grounds of his age, appearance and policies, alongside the headline "Do You Really Want This Old Fool To Run Britain?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StillShines_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-StillShines-56">[56]</a></sup> A year later, in 1984, <i>The Sun</i> made clear its enthusiastic support for the re-election of <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> as president in the USA. Reagan was two weeks off his 74th birthday when he started his second term, in January 1985.</p>
<p>On 1 March 1984 the newspaper extensively quoted a respected American psychiatrist claiming that British left-wing politician <a href="/wiki/Tony_Benn" title="Tony Benn">Tony Benn</a> was "insane", with the psychiatrist discussing various aspects of Benn's supposed pathology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The story, which appeared on the day of the <a href="/wiki/Chesterfield_by-election,_1984" title="Chesterfield by-election, 1984">Chesterfield byelection</a> in which Benn was standing, was discredited when the psychiatrist quoted by <i>The Sun</i> publicly denounced the article and described the false quotes attributed to him as "absurd", <i>The Sun</i> having apparently fabricated the entire piece. The newspaper made frequent scathing attacks on what the paper called the "<a href="/wiki/Loony_left" title="Loony left">loony left</a>" element within the Labour Party<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and on institutions supposedly controlled by it. <a href="/wiki/Ken_Livingstone" title="Ken Livingstone">Ken Livingstone</a>, the leader of the left-wing <a href="/wiki/Greater_London_Council" title="Greater London Council">Greater London Council</a>, was described as "the most odious man in Britain"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> in October 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Sun</i>, during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%931985)" title="UK miners' strike (1984–1985)">miners' strike of 1984–85</a>, supported the police and the Thatcher government against the striking <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Mineworkers_(Great_Britain)" title="National Union of Mineworkers (Great Britain)">NUM</a> miners, and in particular the union's president, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Scargill" title="Arthur Scargill">Arthur Scargill</a>. On 23 May 1984, <i>The Sun</i> prepared a front page with the headline "Mine <a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="Führer">Führer</a>" and a photograph of Scargill with his arm in the air, a pose which made him look as though he was giving a <a href="/wiki/Nazi_salute" title="Nazi salute">Nazi salute</a>. The print workers at <i>The Sun</i> refused to print it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> <i>The Sun</i> strongly supported the April 1986 bombing of <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> by the US, which was launched from British bases. Several civilians were killed during the bombing. Their leader was "Right Ron, Right Maggie".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> That year, Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Clare_Short" title="Clare Short">Clare Short</a> attempted in vain to persuade Parliament to outlaw the pictures on Page Three and gained opprobrium from the newspaper for her stand.</p>
<p>During the 1987 general election, <i>The Sun</i> ran a mock-editorial entitled "Why I'm Backing <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Kinnock</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Stalin</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Murdoch.27s_response">Murdoch's response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Murdoch's response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Murdoch has responded to some of the arguments against the newspaper by saying that critics are "snobs" who want to "impose their tastes on everyone else", while MacKenzie claims the same critics are people who, if they ever had a "popular idea", would have to "go and lie down in a dark room for half an hour". Both have pointed to the huge commercial success of the <i>Sun</i> in this period and its establishment as Britain's top-selling newspaper, claiming that they are "giving the public what they want". This conclusion is disputed by critics. <a href="/wiki/John_Pilger" title="John Pilger">John Pilger</a> has said that a late-1970s edition of the <i>Daily Mirror</i>, which replaced the usual celebrity and domestic political news items with an entire issue devoted to his own front-line reporting of the <a href="/wiki/Cambodian_genocide" title="Cambodian genocide">genocide in Pol Pot's Cambodia</a>, not only outsold <i>The Sun</i> on the day it was issued but became the only edition of the <i>Daily Mirror</i> to ever sell every single copy issued throughout the country, something never achieved by <i>The Sun</i>.</p>
<p>In January 1986 Murdoch shut down the Bouverie Street premises of <i>The Sun</i> and <i>News of the World</i>, and moved operations to the new <a href="/wiki/Wapping" title="Wapping">Wapping</a> complex in East London, substituting the electricians' union for the print unions as his production staff's representatives and greatly reducing the number of staff employed to print the papers; a year-long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picketing_(protest)" title="Picketing (protest)">picket</a> by sacked workers was eventually defeated (see <a href="/wiki/Wapping_dispute" title="Wapping dispute">Wapping dispute</a>).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Freddie_Starr_Ate_My_Hamster.22">"Freddie Starr Ate My Hamster"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title='Edit section: "Freddie Starr Ate My Hamster"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Freddiehamster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="381" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/Freddiehamster.jpg/170px-Freddiehamster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/Freddiehamster.jpg/255px-Freddiehamster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/Freddiehamster.jpg/340px-Freddiehamster.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Freddiehamster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Freddie Starr Ate My Hamster", 13 March 1986</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During this period, <i>The Sun</i> gained a reputation for running sensationalistic stories with questionable veracity. On 13 March 1986, the newspaper published one of its best known headlines: "FREDDIE STARR ATE MY HAMSTER".</p>
<p>The story alleged that British comedian <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Starr" title="Freddie Starr">Freddie Starr</a>, while staying at the home of a writer and old friend of his named Vince McCaffrey and his partner Lea LaSalle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clifford_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clifford-64">[64]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Birchwood" title="Birchwood">Birchwood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>, had, after returning from a performance at a nightclub in the early hours, found little to eat in their house. Starr put LaSalle's pet hamster, she was reported as saying, "between two slices of bread and started eating it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <i>Max Clifford: Read All About It</i>, written by Clifford and Angela Levin, La Salle invented the story out of frustration with Starr who had been working on a book with McCaffrey. She contacted an acquaintance who worked for <i>The Sun</i> in Manchester. The story reportedly delighted MacKenzie, who was keen to run it, and Max Clifford, who had been Starr's public relations agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clifford_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clifford-64">[64]</a></sup> Starr had to be persuaded that the apparent revelation would not damage him; the attention helped to revive his career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In his 2001 autobiography <i>Unwrapped</i>, Starr wrote that the incident was a complete fabrication: "I have never eaten or even nibbled a live hamster, gerbil, guinea pig, mouse, shrew, vole or any other small mammal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Elton_John_and_other_celebrities">Elton John and other celebrities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Elton John and other celebrities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Fuelled by MacKenzie's preoccupation with the subject, stories in <i>The Sun</i> insinuated and spread rumours about the sexual orientation of famous people, especially pop stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eventually resulting in 17 libel writs in total, <i>The Sun</i> ran a series of false stories about the pop musician <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a> from 25 February 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> They began with an invented account of the singer having sexual relationships with rent boys. The singer-songwriter was abroad on the day indicated in the story, as former <i>Sun</i> journalist <a href="/wiki/John_Blake_(journalist)" title="John Blake (journalist)">John Blake</a>, recently poached by the <i>Daily Mirror</i>, soon discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> After further stories, in September 1987, <i>The Sun</i> accused John of having his <a href="/wiki/Rottweiler" title="Rottweiler">Rottweiler</a> guard dogs' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical_debarking" title="Surgical debarking">voice boxes surgically removed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chippindale322_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chippindale322-71">[71]</a></sup> In November, the <i>Daily Mirror</i> found their rival's only source for the rent boy story and he admitted it was a totally fictitious concoction created for money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The inaccurate story about his dogs, actually <a href="/wiki/German_Shepherd" title="German Shepherd">Alsatians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chippindale322_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chippindale322-71">[71]</a></sup> put pressure on <i>The Sun</i>, and John received £1 million in an out of court settlement, then the largest damages payment in British history. <i>The Sun</i> ran a front-page apology on 12 December 1988, under the banner headline "SORRY, ELTON".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In May 1987, gay men were offered free one-way airline tickets to Norway to leave Britain for good: "Fly Away Gays - And We Will Pay" was the paper's headline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Gay Church of England clergymen were described in one headline in November 1987 as "Pulpit poofs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Television personality <a href="/wiki/Piers_Morgan" title="Piers Morgan">Piers Morgan</a>, a former editor of the <i>Daily Mirror</i> and of <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s "Bizarre" pop column, has said that during the late 1980s, at Kelvin MacKenzie's behest, he was ordered to speculate on the sexuality of male pop stars for a feature headlined "The Poofs of Pop".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan05_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan05-76">[76]</a></sup> He also recalls MacKenzie headlining a January 1989 story about the first same-sex kiss on the BBC television soap opera <i><a href="/wiki/EastEnders" title="EastEnders">EastEnders</a></i> "EastBenders",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan05_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan05-76">[76]</a></sup> describing the kiss between <a href="/wiki/Colin_Russell_(EastEnders)" title="Colin Russell (EastEnders)">Colin Russell</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guido_Smith" title="Guido Smith">Guido Smith</a> as "a homosexual love scene between yuppie poofs ... when millions of children were watching".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1990, the Press Council adjudicated against <i>The Sun</i> and columnist <a href="/wiki/Garry_Bushell" title="Garry Bushell">Garry Bushell</a> for their use of derogatory terminology about gays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="AIDS">AIDS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: AIDS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i>The Sun</i> responded to the health crisis on 8 May 1983 with the headline: "US Gay Blood Plague Kills Three in Britain".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 17 November 1989, <i>The Sun</i> headlined a page 2 news story titled "STRAIGHT SEX CANNOT GIVE YOU AIDS – OFFICIAL."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-truth_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-truth-80">[80]</a></sup> <i>The Sun</i> favourably cited the opinions of <a href="/wiki/Baron_Kilbracken" title="Baron Kilbracken">Lord Kilbracken</a>, a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-Party_Parliamentary_Group" title="All-Party Parliamentary Group">All Parliamentary Group on AIDS</a>. Lord Kilbracken said that only one person out of the 2,372 individuals with <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> mentioned in a specific <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Health_(United_Kingdom)" title="Department of Health (United Kingdom)">Department of Health</a> report was not a member of a "high risk group", such as homosexuals and recreational drug users. <i>The Sun</i> also ran an editorial further arguing that "At last the truth can be told ... the risk of catching AIDS if you are heterosexual is 'statistically invisible'. In other words, impossible. So now we know – everything else is homosexual propaganda." Although many other British press services covered Lord Kilbracken's public comments, none of them made the argument that the <i>Sun</i> did in its editorial and none of them presented Lord Kilbracken's ideas without context or criticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-truth_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-truth-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics stated that both <i>The Sun</i> and Lord Kilbracken <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cherry_picking_(fallacy)" title="Cherry picking (fallacy)">cherry-picked</a> the results from one specific study while ignoring other data reports on <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV infection</a> and not just AIDS infection, which the critics viewed as unethical politicisation of a medical issue. Lord Kilbracken himself criticised <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> editorial and the headline of its news story; he stated that while he thought that gay people were more at risk of developing AIDS it was still wrong to imply that no one else could catch the disease. The <a href="/wiki/Press_Council_(UK)" title="Press Council (UK)">Press Council</a> condemned <i>The Sun</i> for committing what it called a "gross distortion". <i>The Sun</i> later ran an apology, which they ran on Page 28. Journalist <a href="/wiki/David_Randall" title="David Randall">David Randall</a> argued in the textbook <i>The Universal Journalist</i> that <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> story was one of the worst cases of journalistic malpractice in recent history, putting its own readers in harm's way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-truth_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-truth-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Hillsborough_disaster_and_its_aftermath">The Hillsborough disaster and its aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The Hillsborough disaster and its aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hillsborough_disaster#The_Sun" title="Hillsborough disaster">Hillsborough disaster § The Sun</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="381" data-file-width="305" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg/170px-Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg/255px-Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/67/Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> front page on 19 April 1989. The allegations were later proven to be entirely false, with the Sun later admitting their decision to publish the allegations was the "blackest day in this newspaper's history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="311" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg/170px-The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg/255px-The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Poster urging the Liverpool public not to purchase <i>The Sun</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the end of the decade, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s coverage of the Hillsborough football stadium disaster in <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a> on 15 April 1989, in which 96 people died as a result of their injuries, proved to be, as the paper later admitted, the "most terrible" blunder in its history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under a front page headline "The Truth", the paper printed allegations provided to them that some fans picked the pockets of crushed victims, that others urinated on members of the emergency services as they tried to help and that some even assaulted a police constable "whilst he was administering the kiss of life to a patient."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Despite the headline, written by <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_MacKenzie" title="Kelvin MacKenzie">Kelvin MacKenzie</a>, the story was based on allegations either by unnamed and unattributable sources, or hearsay accounts of what named individuals had said – a fact made clear to MacKenzie by Harry Arnold, the reporter who wrote the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brook2005_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-brook2005-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>The front page caused outrage in <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>, where the paper lost more than three-quarters of its estimated 55,000 daily sales and still sells poorly in the city more than 25 years later (around 12,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brook2005_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-brook2005-85">[85]</a></sup> It is unavailable in parts of the city, as many newsagents refuse to stock it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SunLiverpool_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-SunLiverpool-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Publican_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Publican-87">[87]</a></sup> It was revealed in a documentary called <i>Alexei Sayle's Liverpool</i>, aired in September 2008, that many Liverpudlians will not even take the newspaper for free, and those who do may simply burn or tear it up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Local people refer to the paper as "The Scum" with campaigners believing it handicapped their fight for justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 7 July 2004, in response to verbal attacks in Liverpool on <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Rooney" title="Wayne Rooney">Wayne Rooney</a>, just before his transfer from <a href="/wiki/Everton_F.C." title="Everton F.C.">Everton</a> to <a href="/wiki/Manchester_United_F.C." title="Manchester United F.C.">Manchester United</a>, who had sold his life story to <i>The Sun</i>, the paper devoted a full-page editorial to an apology for the "awful error" of its Hillsborough coverage and argued that Rooney (who was only three years old at the time of Hillsborough) should not be punished for its "past sins". In January 2005, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> managing editor <a href="/wiki/Graham_Dudman" title="Graham Dudman">Graham Dudman</a> admitting the Hillsborough coverage was "the worst mistake in our history", added: "What we did was a terrible mistake. It was a terrible, insensitive, horrible article, with a dreadful headline; but what we'd also say is: we have apologised for it, and the entire senior team here now is completely different from the team that put the paper out in 1989."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2006, Kelvin MacKenzie, <i>Sun</i> editor at the time of the Hillsborough disaster, returned to the paper as a columnist. Furthermore, on 11 January 2007, MacKenzie stated, while a panellist on BBC1's <i><a href="/wiki/Question_Time_(TV_series)" title="Question Time (TV series)">Question Time</a></i>, that the apology he made about the coverage was a hollow one, forced upon him by <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Murdoch" title="Rupert Murdoch">Rupert Murdoch</a>. MacKenzie further claimed he was not sorry "for telling the truth" but he admitted that he did not know whether some Liverpool fans urinated on the police, or robbed victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 12 September 2012, following the publication of the official report into the disaster using previously withheld Government papers which officially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exonerated" title="Exonerated">exonerated</a> the Liverpool fans present, MacKenzie issued the following statement:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Today I offer my profuse apologies to the people of Liverpool for that headline. I too was totally misled. Twenty three years ago I was handed a piece of copy from a reputable news agency in Sheffield [White's] in which a senior police officer and a senior local MP [Sheffield Hallam MP <a href="/wiki/Irvine_Patnick" title="Irvine Patnick">Irvine Patnick</a>] were making serious allegations against fans in the stadium. I had absolutely no reason to believe that these authority figures would lie and deceive over such a disaster. As the Prime Minister has made clear these allegations were wholly untrue and were part of a concerted plot by police officers to discredit the supporters thereby shifting the blame for the tragedy from themselves. It has taken more than two decades, 400,000 documents and a two-year inquiry to discover to my horror that it would have been far more accurate had I written the headline "The Lies" rather than "The Truth". I published in good faith and I am sorry that it was so wrong.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Trevor Hicks, chairman of the Hillsborough Family Support Group, rejected Mr MacKenzie's apology as "too little, too late", calling him "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lowlife" title="Lowlife">lowlife</a>, clever lowlife, but lowlife".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the publication of the report <i>The Sun</i> apologised on its front page, under the headline "The Real Truth". With the newspaper's editor at the time, Dominic Mohan, adding underneath:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It's a version of events that 23 years ago <i>The Sun</i> went along with and for that we're deeply ashamed and profoundly sorry. We've co-operated fully with The Hillsborough Independent Panel and will publish reports of their findings in tomorrow's newspaper. We will also reflect our deep sense of shame".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Liverpool FC supporters and a significant majority of the City of Liverpool's residents have continued to boycott the newspaper as a result of the Hillsborough tragedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_1990s">The 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>The Sun</i> remained loyal to Thatcher right up to her resignation in November 1990,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> despite the party's fall in popularity over the previous year following the introduction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(United_Kingdom)" title="Poll tax (United Kingdom)">poll tax</a> (officially known as the Community Charge). This change to the way local government is funded was vociferously supported by the newspaper, despite widespread opposition, (some from Conservative MPs), which is seen as having contributed to Thatcher's own downfall. The tax was quickly repealed by her successor <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>, whom <i>The Sun</i> initially supported enthusiastically,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> believing he was a radical Thatcherite – despite the economy having <a href="/wiki/Early_1990s_recession" title="Early 1990s recession">entered recession at this time</a>.</p>
<p>On the day of the general election of 9 April 1992, its front-page headline, encapsulating its antipathy towards the Labour leader <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a>, read "If Kinnock wins today, will the last person to leave Britain please turn out the lights". Two days later <i>The Sun</i> was so convinced its front page had swung a close election for the Conservatives it declared "<a href="/wiki/It%27s_The_Sun_Wot_Won_It" title="It's The Sun Wot Won It">It's The Sun Wot Won It</a>".</p>
<p><i>The Sun</i> led with a headline "Now we've all been screwed by the cabinet" with a reference to <a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">Black Wednesday</a> on 17 September 1992, and the exposure a few months earlier of an extra-marital affair in which Cabinet Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Mellor" title="David Mellor">David Mellor</a> was involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> A month later, on 14 October, it attacked <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a> for the mass coal <a href="/wiki/Mine_closure" title="Mine closure">mine closures</a>.</p>
<p>Despite its initial opposition to the closures, until 1997, the newspaper repeatedly called for the implementation of further Thatcherite policies, such as <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a> privatisation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (June 2011)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and social security cutbacks, with leaders such as "Peter Lilley is right, we can't carry on like this".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (June 2011)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The paper showed hostility to the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU) and approval of public spending cuts, tax cuts, and promotion of right-wing ministers to the cabinet, with leaders such as "More of the Redwood, not Deadwood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Sun</i> attacked Labour leader <a href="/wiki/John_Smith_(Labour_Party_leader)" title="John Smith (Labour Party leader)">John Smith</a> in February 1994, for saying that more British troops should be sent to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosnia" title="Bosnia">Bosnia</a>. <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> comment was that "The only serious radicals in British politics these days are the likes of Redwood, Lilley and Portillo".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (June 2011)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It also gradually expressed its bitter disillusionment with John Major as Prime Minister, with leaders such as "What fools we were to back John Major".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 1994 and 1996, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> circulation peaked. Its highest average sale was in the week ending 16 July 1994, when the daily figure was 4,305,957. The highest ever one-day sale was on 18 November 1995 (4,889,118), although the cover price had been cut to 10p. The highest ever one-day sale at full price was on 30 March 1996 (4,783,359).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 22 January 1997, <i>The Sun</i> accused the shadow chancellor <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> of stealing the Conservatives' ideas by declaring, "If all he is offering is Conservative financial restraint, why not vote for the real thing?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> and called the planned windfall tax, which was later imposed by the Labour government, "wrongheaded".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In February 1997 it told Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> MP to stand down for supporting a national minimum wage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_for_New_Labour">Support for New Labour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Support for New Labour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i>The Sun</i> switched support to the Labour party on 18 March 1997, six weeks before the General Election victory which saw the <a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> become Prime Minister with a large parliamentary majority, despite the paper having attacked Blair and New Labour up to a month earlier. Its front page headline read THE SUN BACKS BLAIR and its front page editorial made clear that while it still opposed some New Labour policies, such as the minimum wage and devolution, it believed Blair to be "the breath of fresh air this great country needs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive1997_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive1997-107">[107]</a></sup> John Major's Conservatives, it said, were "tired, divided and rudderless".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive1997_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-archive1997-107">[107]</a></sup> Blair, who had radically altered his party's image and policies, noting the influence the paper could have over its readers' political thinking, had courted it (and Murdoch) for some time by granting exclusive interviews and writing columns.</p>
<p>In exchange for Rupert Murdoch's support, Blair agreed not to join the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">European Exchange Rate Mechanism</a> – which John Major had <a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">withdrawn the country from</a> in September 1992 after barely two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Cabinet Minister <a href="/wiki/Peter_Mandelson" title="Peter Mandelson">Peter Mandelson</a> was "outed" by <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Parris" title="Matthew Parris">Matthew Parris</a> (a former <i>Sun</i> columnist) on BBC TV's <i><a href="/wiki/Newsnight" title="Newsnight">Newsnight</a></i> in November 1998. Misjudging public response, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> editor David Yelland demanded to know in a front page editorial whether Britain was governed by a "gay mafia" of a "closed world of men with a mutual self-interest". Three days later the paper apologised in another editorial which said <i>The Sun</i> would never again reveal a person's sexuality unless it could be defended on the grounds of "overwhelming public interest".</p>
<p>In 2003 the paper was accused of racism by the Government over its criticisms of what it perceived as the "open door" policy on immigration. The attacks came from the Prime Minister's press spokesman <a href="/wiki/Alastair_Campbell" title="Alastair Campbell">Alastair Campbell</a> and the Home Secretary <a href="/wiki/David_Blunkett" title="David Blunkett">David Blunkett</a> (later a <i>Sun</i> columnist). The paper rebutted the claim, believing that it was not racist to suggest that a "tide" of unchecked illegal immigrants was increasing the risk of terrorist attacks and infectious diseases. It did not help its argument by <a href="/wiki/Publishing" title="Publishing">publishing</a> a front page story on 4 July 2003, under the headline "Swan Bake", which claimed that asylum seekers were slaughtering and eating swans. It later proved to have no basis in fact. Subsequently, <i>The Sun</i> published a follow-up headlined "Now they're after our fish!". Following a <a href="/wiki/Press_Complaints_Commission" title="Press Complaints Commission">Press Complaints Commission</a> adjudication a "clarification" was eventually printed, on page 41.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In 2005 <i>The Sun</i> published photographs of Prince Harry sporting a Nazi costume to a fancy dress party. The photographs caused outrage across the world and <a href="/wiki/Clarence_House" title="Clarence House">Clarence House</a> was forced to issue a statement in response apologising for any offence or embarrassment caused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite being a persistent critic of some of the government's policies, the paper supported Labour in both subsequent elections the party won. For the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005 general election</a>, <i>The Sun</i> backed Blair and Labour for a third consecutive election win and vowed to give him "one last chance" to fulfil his promises, despite berating him for several weaknesses including a failure to control immigration. However, it did speak of its hope that the Conservatives (led by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Howard" title="Michael Howard">Michael Howard</a>) would one day be fit for a return to government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StillShines_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-StillShines-56">[56]</a></sup> This election (Blair had declared it would be his last as prime minister) resulted in Labour's third successive win but with a much reduced majority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Editorial_and_production_issues_in_the_2000s">Editorial and production issues in the 2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Editorial and production issues in the 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potters_Road_shops,_Barnet_(cropped).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1980" data-file-width="2338" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG/220px-Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG/330px-Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG/440px-Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potters_Road_shops,_Barnet_(cropped).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Sun</i>-branded newsagent shop</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Rebekah_Brooks" title="Rebekah Brooks">Rebekah Wade</a> (now Brooks) became editor in 2003, it was thought Page 3 might be dropped. Wade had tried to persuade <a href="/wiki/David_Yelland_(journalist)" title="David Yelland (journalist)">David Yelland</a>, her immediate predecessors in the job, to scrap the feature, but a model who shared her first name was used on her first day in the post.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 22 September 2003 the newspaper appeared to misjudge the public mood surrounding mental health, as well as its affection for former world heavyweight champion boxer <a href="/wiki/Frank_Bruno" title="Frank Bruno">Frank Bruno</a>, who had been admitted to hospital, when the headline "Bonkers Bruno Locked Up" appeared on the front page of early editions. The adverse reaction, once the paper had hit the streets on the evening of 21 September, led to the headline being changed for the paper's second edition to the more sympathetic "Sad Bruno In Mental Home".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Sun</i> has been openly antagonistic towards other European nations, particularly the French and Germans. During the 1980s and 1990s, the nationalities were routinely described in copy and headlines as "frogs", "krauts" or "hun". As the paper is opposed to the EU it has referred to foreign leaders who it deemed hostile to the UK in unflattering terms. Former President <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Chirac" title="Jacques Chirac">Jacques Chirac</a> of France, for instance, was branded "le Worm". An unflattering picture of German chancellor <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Angela Merkel</a>, taken from the rear, bore the headline "I'm Big in the Bumdestag" (17 April 2006).</p>
<p>Although <i>The Sun</i> was outspoken against the racism directed at <a href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> actress <a href="/wiki/Shilpa_Shetty" title="Shilpa Shetty">Shilpa Shetty</a> on television reality show <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celebrity_Big_Brother_2007_(UK)" title="Celebrity Big Brother 2007 (UK)">Celebrity Big Brother</a></i> during 2007, the paper captioned a picture on its website, from a Bollywood-themed pop video by <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Duff" title="Hilary Duff">Hilary Duff</a>, "Hilary <a href="/wiki/Papadum" title="Papadum">PoppaDuff</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> a very similar insult to that directed at Shetty.</p>
<p>On 7 January 2009, <i>The Sun</i> ran an exclusive front page story claiming that participants in a discussion on Ummah.com, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Muslim" title="British Muslim">British Muslim</a> <a href="/wiki/Internet_forum" title="Internet forum">internet forum</a>, had made a "hate hit list" of <a href="/wiki/British_Jews" title="British Jews">British Jews</a> to be targeted by extremists over the <a href="/wiki/Gaza_War_(2008%E2%80%9309)" title="Gaza War (2008–09)">Gaza War</a>. It was claimed that "Those listed [on the forum] should treat it very seriously. Expect a hate campaign and intimidation by 20 or 30 thugs." The UK magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Private_Eye" title="Private Eye">Private Eye</a></i> claimed that <a href="/wiki/Glen_Jenvey" title="Glen Jenvey">Glen Jenvey</a>, a man quoted by <i>The Sun</i> as a terrorism expert, who had been posting to the forum under the pseudonym "Abuislam", was the only forum member promoting a hate campaign while other members promoted peaceful <a href="/wiki/Advocacy" title="Advocacy">advocacy</a>, such as writing "polite letters". The story has since been removed from <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> website following complaints to the UK's <a href="/wiki/Press_Complaints_Commission" title="Press Complaints Commission">Press Complaints Commission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 9 December 2010, <i>The Sun</i> published a front-page story claiming that terrorist group <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> had threatened a terrorist attack on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Granada_Television" title="Granada Television">Granada Television</a> in Manchester to disrupt the episode of the soap opera <i><a href="/wiki/Coronation_Street" title="Coronation Street">Coronation Street</a></i> to be transmitted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronation_Street:_50th_anniversary_live_episode" title="Coronation Street: 50th anniversary live episode">live</a> that evening. The paper cited unnamed sources, claiming "cops are throwing a ring of steel around tonight's live episode of <i>Coronation Street</i> over fears it has been targeted by Al-Qaeda."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Later that morning, however, <a href="/wiki/Greater_Manchester_Police" title="Greater Manchester Police">Greater Manchester Police</a> categorically denied having "been made aware of any threat from Al-Qaeda or any other proscribed organisation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deny_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-deny-117">[117]</a></sup> <i>The Sun</i> published a small correction on 28 December, admitting "that while cast and crew were subject to full body searches, there was no specific threat from Al-Qaeda as we reported."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The apology had been negotiated by the Press Complaints Commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> For the day following the <a href="/wiki/2011_Norway_attacks" title="2011 Norway attacks">2011 Norway attacks</a>, <i>The Sun</i> produced an early edition blaming the massacre on al-Qaeda. Later the perpetrator was revealed to be <a href="/wiki/Anders_Behring_Breivik" title="Anders Behring Breivik">Anders Behring Breivik</a>, a Norwegian nationalist.</p>
<p>In January 2008 the <a href="/wiki/Wapping" title="Wapping">Wapping</a> presses printed <i>The Sun</i> for the last time and London printing was transferred to <a href="/wiki/Waltham_Cross" title="Waltham Cross">Waltham Cross</a> in the Borough of <a href="/wiki/Broxbourne" title="Broxbourne">Broxbourne</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hertfordshire" title="Hertfordshire">Hertfordshire</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> where News International had built what is claimed to be the largest printing centre in Europe with 12 presses. The site also produces <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">Sunday Times</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">Daily Telegraph</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Telegraph" title="The Sunday Telegraph">Sunday Telegraph</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal_Europe" title="The Wall Street Journal Europe">Wall Street Journal Europe</a></i> (also a Murdoch newspaper), the <i><a href="/wiki/London_Evening_Standard" title="London Evening Standard">London Evening Standard</a></i>, and local papers. Northern printing had earlier been switched to a new plant at Knowsley on Merseyside and the <i>Scottish Sun</i> to another new plant at Motherwell near Glasgow. The three print centres represent a £600 million investment by NI and allowed all the titles to be produced with every page in full colour from 2008. The Waltham Cross plant is capable of producing one million copies an hour of a 120-page tabloid newspaper.</p>
<p>In early 2011, the company vacated the Wapping complex, which in November 2011 was put on the market for a reputed £200 million. In May 2012, it was reported the Wapping site had been sold for £150 million to St George, part of <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Group_Holdings" title="Berkeley Group Holdings">Berkeley Group Holdings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wapping_Sale_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wapping_Sale-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2009:_The_Sun_returns_to_the_Conservatives">2009: <i>The Sun</i> returns to the Conservatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 2009: The Sun returns to the Conservatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Murdoch#Political_activities" title="Rupert Murdoch">Political activities of Rupert Murdoch</a></div>
<p>Politically, the paper's stance was less clear under Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> who succeeded Blair in June 2007. Its editorials were critical of many of Brown's policies and often more supportive of those of Conservative leader <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>. <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Murdoch" title="Rupert Murdoch">Rupert Murdoch</a>, head of <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s parent company News Corporation, speaking at a 2007 meeting with the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> Select Committee on Communications, which was investigating media ownership and the news, said that he acts as a "traditional proprietor". This means he exercises editorial control on major issues such as which political party to back in a general election or which policy to adopt on Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parliament070917_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parliament070917-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
