<p>With "<a href="/wiki/Broken_Britain" title="Broken Britain">Broken Britain</a>" controversies on issues like crime, immigration and public service failures in the news, on 30 September 2009, following Brown's speech at the Labour Party Conference, <i>The Sun</i>, under the banner "Labour's Lost It", announced that it no longer supported the Labour Party:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> "<i>The Sun</i> believes – and prays – that the Conservative leadership can put the great back into Great Britain", though the <i>Scottish Sun</i> was more equivocal in its editorial.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>That day at the Labour Party Conference, union leader <a href="/wiki/Tony_Woodley" title="Tony Woodley">Tony Woodley</a> responded by ripping up a copy of that edition of <i>The Sun</i>, remarking as he did so in reference to the newspaper's Hillsborough Disaster controversy: "In Liverpool we learnt a long time ago what to do".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> One attack on Gordon Brown backfired at around this time. After criticising him for misspelling a dead soldier's mother's name, <i>The Sun</i> was then forced to apologise for misspelling the same name on their website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Scottish Sun</i> did not back either Labour or the Conservatives, with its editorial stating it was "yet to be convinced" by the Conservative opposition, and editor David Dinsmore asking in an interview "what is David Cameron going to do for Scotland?".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Dinsmore also stated that the paper supported the Union, and was unlikely to back the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a>.</p>
<p>During the campaign for the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 general election</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> ran ads declaring that "Rupert Murdoch won't decide this election – you will." In response <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Murdoch_(media_executive)" title="James Murdoch (media executive)">James Murdoch</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rebekah_Wade" title="Rebekah Wade">Rebekah Wade</a> "appeared unannounced and uninvited on the editorial floor" of the <i>Independent</i>, and had an energetic conversation with its editor <a href="/wiki/Simon_Kelner" title="Simon Kelner">Simon Kelner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Several days later the <i>Independent</i> reported <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> failure to report its own <a href="/wiki/YouGov" title="YouGov">YouGov</a> poll result which said that "if people thought Mr Clegg's party had a significant chance of winning the election" the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats" title="Liberal Democrats">Liberal Democrats</a> would win 49% of the vote, and with it a landslide majority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ind230410_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-ind230410-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>On <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">election day</a> (6 May 2010), <i>The Sun</i> urged its readers to vote for David Cameron's "modern and positive" Conservatives in order to save Britain from "disaster" which the paper thought the country would face if the Labour government was re-elected. The election ended in the first <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a> after an election for <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">36 years</a>, with the Tories gaining the most seats and votes but being 20 seats short of an overall majority. They finally came to power on 11 May when Gordon Brown stepped down as prime minister, paving the way for David Cameron to become prime minister by forming a coalition with the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats" title="Liberal Democrats">Liberal Democrats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 24 August 2012, <i>The Sun</i> sparked a controversy when it published photos of <a href="/wiki/Prince_Harry" title="Prince Harry">Prince Harry</a> taken in a private situation with friends while on holiday in <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_Valley" title="Las Vegas Valley">Las Vegas</a>, USA. While other British newspapers had not published the photos in deference to the privacy of members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Royal_Family" title="British Royal Family">Royal Family</a>, editorial staff of <i>The Sun</i> claimed it was a move to test Britain's perception of freedom of the press. In the photos, which were published on the Internet worldwide, Prince Harry was naked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_2010">Since 2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Since 2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fallout_from_the_News_of_the_World_scandal">Fallout from the <i>News of the World</i> scandal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fallout from the News of the World scandal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The-Sun-on-Sunday-cover-specimen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="300" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/The-Sun-on-Sunday-cover-specimen.jpg/240px-The-Sun-on-Sunday-cover-specimen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/64/The-Sun-on-Sunday-cover-specimen.jpg 1.5x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The-Sun-on-Sunday-cover-specimen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Sun on Sunday</i> front page</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/News_of_the_World_phone_hacking_affair" title="News of the World phone hacking affair"><i>News of the World</i> phone hacking affair</a> that led to the closure of that paper on 10 July 2011, there was speculation that News International would launch a Sunday edition of <i>The Sun</i> to replace the <i>News of the World</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> The internet URLs <i>sunonsunday.co.uk</i>, <i>thesunonsunday.co.uk</i> and <i>thesunonsunday.com</i> were registered on 5 July 2011 by News International Newspapers Limited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> A similar URL <i>sunonsunday.com</i> is not affiliated, having been registered in Italy on 24 September 2007.</p>
<p>On 18 July 2011, the <a href="/wiki/LulzSec" title="LulzSec">LulzSec</a> group hacked <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> website, where they posted a fake news story of Rupert Murdoch's death before redirecting the website to their Twitter page. The group also targeted the website of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arthur_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arthur-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>A reporter working for <i>The Sun</i> was arrested and taken to a south-west London police station on 4 November 2011. The man was the sixth person to be arrested in the UK under the News International related legal probe, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Elveden" title="Operation Elveden">Operation Elveden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> In January 2012, two current and two former employees were arrested. As of 18 January 2013, 22 <i>Sun</i> journalists had been arrested, including their crime reporter Anthony France.</p>
<p>On 28 January 2012, police arrested four current and former staff members of <i>The Sun</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> as part of a probe in which journalists paid police officers for information; a police officer was also arrested in the probe. The <i>Sun</i> staffers arrested were crime editor Mike Sullivan, head of news Chris Pharo, former deputy editor Fergus Shanahan, and former managing editor Graham Dudman, who since became a columnist and media writer. All five arrested were held on suspicion of corruption. Police also searched the offices of News International, the publishers of <i>The Sun</i>, as part of a continuing investigation into the <i>News of the World</i> scandal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 11 February 2012, five senior journalists at <i>The Sun</i> were arrested, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geoff_Webster_(journalist)" title="Geoff Webster (journalist)">deputy editor</a>, as part of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Elveden" title="Operation Elveden">Operation Elveden</a> (the investigation into payments to UK public servants).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coinciding with a visit to <i>The Sun</i> newsroom on 17 February 2012, Murdoch announced via an email that the arrested journalists, who had been suspended, would return to work as nothing had been proved against them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuardRMemail_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-GuardRMemail-9">[9]</a></sup> He also told staff in the email that <i>The Sun on Sunday</i> would be launched "very shortly";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuardRMemail_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-GuardRMemail-9">[9]</a></sup> it was launched on 26 February 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 27 February 2012, the day after the debut of <i>The Sun on Sunday</i>, Deputy Assistant Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Sue_Akers" title="Sue Akers">Sue Akers</a> told the <a href="/wiki/Leveson_Inquiry" title="Leveson Inquiry">Leveson Inquiry</a> that police were investigating a "network of corrupt officials" as part of their inquiries into phone hacking and police corruption. She said evidence suggested a "culture of illegal payments" at <i>The Sun</i> authorised at a senior level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Cup_2014_free_issue">World Cup 2014 free issue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: World Cup 2014 free issue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 12 and 13 June 2014, to tie in with the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/2014_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2014 FIFA World Cup">2014 World Cup</a> football tournament, a free special issue of <i>The Sun</i> was distributed by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a> to 22 million homes in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenslade110614_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade110614-142">[142]</a></sup> The promotion, which did not include a Page 3 topless model, was announced in mid-May and was believed to the first such freesheet issued by a UK national newspaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>The boycott in Merseyside following the newspaper's coverage of the Hillsborough disaster in 1989 meant that copies were not dispatched to areas with a Liverpool postcode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withnall_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Withnall-144">[144]</a></sup> Royal Mail employees in Merseyside and surrounding areas were given special dispensation by their managers to allow them not to handle the publication "on a case by case basis".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withnall_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Withnall-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main party leaders, <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg" title="Nick Clegg">Nick Clegg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a>, were all depicted holding a copy of the special issue in publicity material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Miliband's decision to pose with a copy of <i>The Sun</i> received a strong response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Organisations representing the relatives of Hillsborough victims described Miliband's action as an "absolute disgrace"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC130614_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC130614-148">[148]</a></sup> and he faced criticism too from Liverpool Labour MPs and the city's Labour Mayor, Joe Anderson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> A statement was issued on 13 June explaining that Miliband "was promoting England's bid to win the World Cup", although "he understands the anger that is felt towards the Sun over Hillsborough by many people in Merseyside and he is sorry to those who feel offended."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC130614_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC130614-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Promoted as "an unapologetic celebration of England", the special issue of <i>The Sun</i> ran to 24 pages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenslade110614_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade110614-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Collapse_of_Tulisa_Contostavlos.27_trial_for_drug_offences">Collapse of Tulisa Contostavlos' trial for drug offences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Collapse of Tulisa Contostavlos' trial for drug offences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 2 June 2013, <i>The Sun on Sunday</i> ran a front page story on singer-songwriter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tulisa_Contostavlos" title="Tulisa Contostavlos">Tulisa Contostavlos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenslade_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade-151">[151]</a></sup> The front page read: "Tulisa's cocaine deal shame"; this story was written by <i>The Sun On Sunday</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s undercover reporter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahzer_Mahmood" title="Mahzer Mahmood">Mahzer Mahmood</a>, who had previously worked for the <i>News of the World</i>. It was claimed that Tulisa introduced three film producers (actually Mahmood and two other <i>Sun</i> journalists) to a drug dealer and set up a £800 deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenslade_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade-151">[151]</a></sup> The subterfuge involved conning the singer into believing that she was being considered for a role in an £8 million Bollywood film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>At her subsequent trial, the case against Tulisa collapsed at <a href="/wiki/Southwark_Crown_Court" title="Southwark Crown Court">Southwark Crown Court</a> in July 2014, with the judge commenting that there were "strong grounds" to believe that Mahmood had lied at a pre-trial hearing and tried to manipulate evidence against the co-defendant Tulisa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-153">[153]</a></sup> Tulisa was cleared of supplying <a href="/wiki/Drugs_controlled_by_the_UK_Misuse_of_Drugs_Act" title="Drugs controlled by the UK Misuse of Drugs Act">Class A drugs</a>. After these events, <i>The Sun</i> released a statement saying that the newspaper "takes the Judge's remarks very seriously. Mahmood has been suspended pending an immediate internal investigation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trial_of_staff_for_misconduct_in_a_public_office">Trial of staff for misconduct in a public office</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Trial of staff for misconduct in a public office">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In October 2014, the trial of six senior staff and journalists at <i>The Sun</i> newspaper began. All six were charged with conspiring to commit misconduct in a public office. They included <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s head of news Chris Pharo, who faced six charges, while ex-managing editor <a href="/wiki/Graham_Dudman" title="Graham Dudman">Graham Dudman</a> and ex-<i>Sun</i> deputy news editor Ben O'Driscoll were accused of four charges each. Thames Valley district reporter Jamie Pyatt and picture editor John Edwards were charged with three counts each, while ex-reporter John Troup was accused of two counts. The trial related to illegal payments allegedly made to public officials, with prosecutors saying the men conspired to pay officials from 2002 to 2011, including police, prison officers and soldiers. They were accused of buying confidential information about the Royal Family, public figures and prison inmates. They all denied the charges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> On 16 January 2015, Troup and Edwards were cleared by the jury of all charges against them. The jury also partially cleared O'Driscoll and Dudman but continued deliberating over other counts faced by them, as well as the charges against Pharo and Pyatt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_16_January_2015_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_16_January_2015-156">[156]</a></sup> On 21 January 2015, the jury told the court that it was unable to reach unanimous verdicts on any of the outstanding charges and was told by the judge, Richard Marks, that he would accept <a href="/wiki/Hung_jury#England_and_Wales" title="Hung jury">majority verdicts</a>. Shortly afterwards, one of the jurors sent a note to the judge and was discharged. The judge told the remaining 11 jurors that their colleague had been "feeling unwell and feeling under a great deal of pressure and stress from the situation you are in", and that under the circumstances he was prepared to accept majority verdicts of "11 to zero or 10 to 1".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Guardian_21_Jan_2015_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian_21_Jan_2015-157">[157]</a></sup> On 22 January 2015, the jury was discharged after failing to reach verdicts on the outstanding charges. The <a href="/wiki/Crown_Prosecution_Service" title="Crown Prosecution Service">Crown Prosecution Service</a> (CPS) announced that it would seek a retrial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_22_January_2015_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_22_January_2015-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 6 February 2015, it was announced that Judge Richard Marks was to be replaced by Judge Charles Wide at the retrial. Two days earlier, Marks had emailed counsel for the defendants, telling them: "It has been decided (not by me but by my elders and betters) that I am not going to be doing the retrial". Reporting the decision in UK newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>, Lisa O’Carroll wrote: "Wide is the only judge so far to have presided in a case which has seen a conviction of a journalist in relation to allegations of unlawful payments to public officials for stories. The journalist, who cannot be named for legal reasons, is appealing the verdict". Defence counsel for the four journalists threatened to take the decision to judicial review, with the barrister representing Pharo, Nigel Rumfitt QC, saying: "The way this has come about gives rise to the impression that something has been going on behind the scenes which should not have been going on behind the scenes and which should have been dealt with transparently". He added that the defendants were "extremely concerned" and "entitled" to know why Marks was being replaced by Wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Guardian_6_February_2015_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian_6_February_2015-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a separate trial, <i>Sun</i> reporter Nick Parker was cleared on 9 December 2014 of aiding and abetting misconduct in a public office but found guilty of handling a stolen mobile phone belonging to Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Siobhain_McDonagh" title="Siobhain McDonagh">Siobhain McDonagh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_9_December_2014_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_9_December_2014-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 22 May 2015, <i>Sun</i> reporter Anthony France was found guilty of aiding and abetting misconduct in a public office between 2008 and 2011. France's trial followed the London <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Operation_Elveden" title="Operation Elveden">Operation Elveden</a>, an ongoing investigation into alleged payments to police and officials in exchange for information. He had paid a total of more than £22,000 to PC Timothy Edwards, an anti-terrorism police officer based at Heathrow Airport. The police officer had already pleaded guilty to misconduct in a public office and given a two-year gaol sentence in 2014, but the jury in France's trial was not informed of this. Following the passing of the guilty verdict, the officer leading Operation Elveden, Detective Chief Superintendent Gordon Briggs said France and Edwards had been in a "long-term, corrupt relationship".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_22_May_2015_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_22_May_2015-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>The BBC reported that France was the first journalist to face trial and be convicted under Operation Elveden since the Crown Prosecution Service (CPS) had revised its guidance in April 2015 so that prosecutions would only be brought against journalists who had made payments to police officers over a period of time. As a result of the change in the CPS' policy, charges against several journalists who had made payments to other types of public officials – including civil servants, health workers and prison staff - had been dropped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_22_May_2015_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_22_May_2015-161">[161]</a></sup> In July 2015, <i><a href="/wiki/Private_Eye" title="Private Eye">Private Eye</a></i> magazine reported that, at a costs hearing at the Old Bailey, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> parent company had refused to pay for the prosecution costs relating to France’s trial, leading the presiding judge to express his "considerable disappointment" at this state of affairs. Judge Timothy Pontius said in court that France’s illegal actions had been part of a "clearly recognised procedure at <i>The Sun</i>", adding that, "There can be no doubt that News International bears some measure of moral responsibility if not legal culpability for the acts of the defendant". The <i>Private Eye</i> report noted that despite this <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> parent organisation was "considering disciplinary actions" against France whilst at the same time it was also preparing to bring a case to the <a href="/wiki/Investigatory_Powers_Tribunal" title="Investigatory Powers Tribunal">Investigatory Powers Tribunal</a> against the London <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police Service</a> for its actions relating to him and two other journalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Private_Eye_24_July_2015_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Private_Eye_24_July_2015-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_Page_3_feature_.28January_2015.29">End of the Page 3 feature (January 2015)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: End of the Page 3 feature (January 2015)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i>The Sun</i> defended <a href="/wiki/Page_3" title="Page 3">Page 3</a> for more than 40 years, with (then) editor Dominic Mohan telling the <a href="/wiki/Leveson_Inquiry" title="Leveson Inquiry">Leveson Inquiry</a> into press standards, in February 2012, that "Page 3" was an "innocuous British Institution, regarded with affection and tolerance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohan_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mohan-163">[163]</a></sup> To mark the feature's 40th anniversary, feminist author <a href="/wiki/Germaine_Greer" title="Germaine Greer">Germaine Greer</a> wrote an article in <i>The Sun</i> on 18 November 2010 published under the headline: "If I ask my odd-job man what he gets out of page 3, he tells me simply, 'It cheers me up'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Germaine_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Germaine-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2013, <i>The Irish Sun</i> ended the practice of featuring topless models on Page 3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> The main newspaper was reported to have followed in 2015 with the edition of 16 January supposedly the last to carry such photographs after a report in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> made such an assertion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.27Carroll_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Carroll-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> After substantial coverage in the media about an alleged change in editorial policy, Page 3 returned to its usual format on 22 January 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph2015_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph2015-168">[168]</a></sup> A few hours before the issue was published, the Head of PR at the newspaper said the reputed end of Page 3 had been "speculation" only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apart from the edition of 22 January, the conventional Page 3 feature of a topless model has not returned, and has effectively ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Migrants">Migrants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Migrants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 17 April 2015, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> columnist <a href="/wiki/Katie_Hopkins" title="Katie Hopkins">Katie Hopkins</a> called <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">migrants</a> to Britain "cockroaches" and "feral humans" and said they were "spreading like the norovirus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITV180415_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITV180415-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gander_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gander-172">[172]</a></sup> Her remarks were condemned by the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Human_Rights" title="Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights">United Nations High Commission for Human Rights</a>. In a statement released on 24 April 2015, High Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Prince_Zeid_bin_Ra%27ad" title="Prince Zeid bin Ra'ad">Zeid Ra'ad Al Hussein</a> stated that Hopkins' used "language very similar to that employed by Rwanda's <i><a href="/wiki/Kangura" title="Kangura">Kangura</a></i> newspaper and <a href="/wiki/Radio_T%C3%A9l%C3%A9vision_Libre_des_Mille_Collines" title="Radio Télévision Libre des Mille Collines">Radio Mille Collines</a> during the run up to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rwandan_Genocide" title="Rwandan Genocide">1994 genocide</a>", and noted that both media organisations were subsequently convicted by an international tribunal of public incitement to commit <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Independent_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Independent-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hopkins' column also drew criticism on Twitter, including from <a href="/wiki/Russell_Brand" title="Russell Brand">Russell Brand</a>, to whom Hopkins responded by accusing Brand's "champagne socialist humanity" of neglecting taxpayers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gander_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gander-172">[172]</a></sup> Simon Usborne, writing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i>, compared her use of the word "cockroach" to previous uses by the <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazis</a> and just before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rwandan_Genocide" title="Rwandan Genocide">Rwandan Genocide</a> by its perpetrators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Usborne_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Usborne-174">[174]</a></sup> He suspected that if any other contributor had written the piece it would not have been published and questioned her continued employment by the newspaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Usborne_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-Usborne-174">[174]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zoe_Williams" title="Zoe Williams">Zoe Williams</a> commented in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>: "It is no joke when people start talking like this. We are not 'giving her what she wants' when we make manifest our disgust. It is not a free speech issue. I’m not saying gag her: I’m saying fight her".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Change.org" title="Change.org">Change.org</a> petition was initiated with the aim of getting <i>The Sun</i> to sack Hopkins. By 26 April, it had attracted over 310,000 signatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> In September, <i>The Sun</i> retweeted an earlier comment from Hopkins expressing her disinterest in migrants. The tweet was pulled after the Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> publicly announced Britain would do more to help those seeking asylum in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Brexit.22">"Brexit"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title='Edit section: "Brexit"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 9 March 2016, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s front page proclaimed that Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> was backing "Brexit", a common term for a British withdrawal from the European Union. It claimed that in 2011 at <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a>, while having lunch with Deputy Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg" title="Nick Clegg">Nick Clegg</a>, the monarch criticised the union. Clegg denied that the Queen made such a statement, and a <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a> spokesperson confirmed that a complaint had been made to the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Press_Standards_Organisation" title="Independent Press Standards Organisation">Independent Press Standards Organisation</a> over a breach of guidelines relating to accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Sun</i> officially endorsed the "Leave" campaign in the British <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">referendum to remain in or leave</a> the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> on 23 June 2016, urging its readers to vote for the United Kingdom to leave the EU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The "BeLeave in Britain" front page headline was only present on copies distributed in England and Wales; editions for the rest of the UK (and the Republic of Ireland) led on other topics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Website_redesign">Website redesign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Website redesign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In June 2016, a redesign of <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> website was launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redesign_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-redesign-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Editors">Editors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Editors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sydney_Jacobson" title="Sydney Jacobson">Sydney Jacobson</a> (1964–65, previously editor of the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Herald_(UK_newspaper)" title="Daily Herald (UK newspaper)">Daily Herald</a></i> before the name change)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dick_Dinsdale" title="Dick Dinsdale">Dick Dinsdale</a> (1965–69)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Larry_Lamb_(newspaper_editor)" title="Larry Lamb (newspaper editor)">Larry Lamb</a> (1969–72)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bernard_Shrimsley" title="Bernard Shrimsley">Bernard Shrimsley</a> (1972–75; Lamb was "editorial director", supervising both the <i>Sun</i> and <i>News of the World</i>)</li>
<li>Larry Lamb (1975–80; Lamb took an enforced six-month sabbatical before being sacked by Murdoch)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kelvin_MacKenzie" title="Kelvin MacKenzie">Kelvin MacKenzie</a> (1981–94)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Stuart_Higgins" title="Stuart Higgins">Stuart Higgins</a> (1994–98)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/David_Yelland_(journalist)" title="David Yelland (journalist)">David Yelland</a> (1998–2003)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rebekah_Brooks" title="Rebekah Brooks">Rebekah Wade</a> (2003–09)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dominic_Mohan" title="Dominic Mohan">Dominic Mohan</a> (2009–2013)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/David_Dinsmore" title="David Dinsmore">David Dinsmore</a> (2013–2015)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC2106_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2106-182">[182]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tony_Gallagher" title="Tony Gallagher">Tony Gallagher</a> (2015–)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PG020915_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-PG020915-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_versions">Other versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Other versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Scottish_Sun"><i>The Scottish Sun</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: The Scottish Sun">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Scottish edition of <i>The Sun</i> launched in 1987, known as <i>The Scottish Sun</i>. Based in Glasgow, it duplicates much of the content of the main edition but with alternative coverage of Scottish news and sport. The launch editor was Jack Irvine who had been recruited from the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Record_(Scotland)" title="Daily Record (Scotland)">Daily Record</a></i>.</p>
<p>In the early 1990s, the Scottish edition declared support for the pro-independence <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a>. At the time the paper elsewhere continued to support the Conservatives, who were then becoming an increasingly marginalised force in Scotland.</p>
<p>However, the <i>Scottish Sun</i> had performed a major U-turn by the time of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_election,_2007" title="Scottish Parliament election, 2007">2007 Scottish parliamentary election</a>, in which its front page featured a hangman's noose in the shape of an SNP logo, stating "Vote SNP today and you put Scotland's head in the noose".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> The <i>Scottish Sun</i> voiced its support for the SNP in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_general_election,_2011" title="Scottish Parliament general election, 2011">2011 parliamentary election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although it expressed some support for <a href="/wiki/Alex_Salmond" title="Alex Salmond">Alex Salmond</a>, then <a href="/wiki/First_Minister_of_Scotland" title="First Minister of Scotland">First Minister</a> and the SNP's leader, <i>The Scottish Sun</i> took a neutral stance on the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence_referendum,_2014" title="Scottish independence referendum, 2014">referendum on Scottish independence</a>. On 17 September, the day before the poll, an editorial commented: "What we cannot do is tell you how we think you should vote".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 general election</a>, <i>The Scottish Sun</i> urged its readers to back the SNP. While in England and Wales, the paper saw a vote for the Conservatives as a means to "stop [the] SNP running the country", the edition north of the border said the SNP would "fight harder for Scotland's interests at Westminster".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withnall_144-2"><a href="#cite_note-Withnall-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Irish_Sun"><i>The Irish Sun</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: The Irish Sun">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Irish edition of the newspaper, based in Dublin, is known as the <i>Irish Sun</i>, with a regional sub-edition for <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> where it is mastheaded as <i>The Sun</i>, based in Belfast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> The Republic of Ireland edition shares some content – namely glamour and showbiz – with the editions published in Great Britain, but has mainly Irish news and editorial content, as well as sport and advertising.</p>
<p>It often views stories in a very different light to those being reported in the UK editions. Editions of the paper in Great Britain described the film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wind_That_Shakes_the_Barley_(film)" title="The Wind That Shakes the Barley (film)">The Wind That Shakes the Barley</a></i> (2006) as being "designed to drag the reputation of our nation through the mud" and "the most pro-IRA ever";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> conversely, the Republic of Ireland edition praised the film and described it as giving "the Brits a tanning".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Irish Sun</i>, unlike its sister papers in Great Britain, did not have a designated website until late 2012. An unaffiliated news site with the name <i>Irish Sun</i> has been in operation since mid-2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irish_Sun_About_Us_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Sun_About_Us-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
